BLACK HILLS DISTRICT
AROUSED. BY MURDER
buildings also have Interior fire plugs.
During the day when the campus. Is
crowded and the rooms are full of stu
dents the chances of a serious fire are not
now considered great, but at night time,
with only one .watchman to patrol the
grounds, the possibility of fire is a con
tinual terror to university men. In la
boratories where chemicals are kept and
where careless students are apt to leave
a burner or a jet lighted, fires areiiable
to occur, and do more often than the
general public is aware. Fortunately,
heretofore there has generally been a
watchman or some one else on hand to
extinguish the blaze or to summon" help
before great damage was done, and the
fact of a fire is not made known until it
leaks out weeks afterward.
This good fortune .is not always the
case, however, for in 1S97 a morning fire,
with plenty of tire-fighters on the ground,
burned the agricultural building to the
ground floor. Many of Its valuable collec
tions and books were lost. About two
years ago one rcom in the civil engineer
ing building was gutted before the fire
was stopped, and more recently the botan
ical building was damaged by a daytime,
blaze.
In West Berkeley new manufactories
are going up without fire protection, and
in Cast Berkeley there are blocks and
blocks of $10,000 residences depending on a
Contra Costa water pressure for safety
from fire. Few towns have had a faster
steady growth than this city, and there is
perhaps no place in California wherte more
and better homes are being built at the
present time. It is this rapid and healthy
growth that has made Berkeley spring
from a suburban town Into a city with
all a city's, needs and requirements.
LABOR PAET"! LAUNCHED
BY OAKLAND WORZINGMEN
Intend to Put a Complete Ticket in
the Field at the Coming
. - Election. ' â¢ . '
OAKLAND. Aug. lS.-The United Labor
party of the State of California wa3
formed in Oakland this afternoon. It was
launched with a declaration of principles,
a central organization and about 100 mem
bers who signed the declaration of princi
ples,that announced a 1 determination to
form a new party that would put a com
plete State ticket in the field.
The movement has been on foot for sev
eral weeks, but It did not take definite
shape until to-day, when a call was cir
culated for a meeting to be held in Koh
ler & Chase Hall. The call was circulated
among the laboring men, particularly the
union men, though the public was in
vited. The result was between fifty and
seventy-five men, nearly all members of
unions, who were present when C. D.
Rogers called the meeting to order. Mr.
Rogers is the State president of the
American Federation of Labor and lives
in Oakland. Mr. Rogers and all of those
who were present say that they were
there in their capacity as citizens and not
as members of the unions.
There were a number of addresses by J.
E. Holmes. P. B. Preble. J. Reboli, D. A.
Shannon and others, at the end of which
it was decided by the meeting to form a
political party that should embrace the
entire State and should have for Its ob
ject the nomination of a complete State
ticket next year. A committee composed
of P. B. Preble. C. I. Jacobs and C. A.
Sinclair was "appointed to embody that
idea in the form of a declaration of prin
ciples, and the following was the result:
Recognizing that the legislative functions of
our municipal. State and national Governments
are being usurped by organized capital, and
that monopoly and privileges control the indus
trial destinies of the* commonwealth through
existing laws and political organizations; and
believing that there is no hope of securing leg
islation favorable to the laboring people from
said political organizations, the undersigned
electors of Alarncda County, California, hereby
signify their intention of forming a new party,
to be known as the United Labor party of the
State of California, for the purpose of nominat
ing a full State ticket to be voted for at the
next general election, upon a platform pledg
ing all nominees' primarily to work and vote
for a law providing for direct legislation and
proportional representation, with such other de
mands as may be deemed expedient at the time
when the convention is held for the purpose of
nominating said ticket.
It was then decided to have a central
organization, that would take the form of
a club rather than a political central com
mittee. This club is expected to be the
nucleus of the political party, and will ex
tend the organization to San Francisco
and other places. To this end a special
organizing committee was appointed,
every member of which comes from a dif
ferent union. The regular officers and
committees are as follows:
President. J. E. Holmes: vice president, D. A.
Shannon: secretary. J. Reboli: treasurer. F. W.
Downing: organizing committeeâ C. A. Two
mey, J. Ward. C. D. Rogers, Fred Murdock, J.
Dickie. H. Cornwall. J. Callahan and G. Manes:
committee on by-laws â P. B. Preble, J. Flick,
Fred Murdock. J. Atadden and G. Manes; press
committeeâ D. A. Shannon, P. B. Preble and J.
E. Holme?. .

BRITISH ADMIRALTY WILL
USE WIRELESS TELEGRAPHY
HAVANA, Aug. Â¦ 18.â Chief Surgeon Ha
vard . announced to-day that the experi
ments of the division on the propagation
of yellow fever, so far as these involved the
mosquito test, will be discontinued. This
decision Â¦was taken because one of the
non-immunes who was recently bitten by
an infected mosquito died of yellow fever
to-day. The man, who was a Spaniard,
desired to become an immune and there
fore allowed himself to be bitten by an
infected mosquito. Another man who was
bitten is also .suffering from a very bad
case. Both were bitten by insects which
had been set apart for the experiments of
Dr. Caldaz, the Brazilian expert, who is
arranging to demonstrate the alleged ef
ficiency of a serum as a preventive
against yellow fever.
According to Major Havard the cases
due to mosquito infection prior to the
latest two were light, out the matter has
assumed a more dangerous form than, the
first experiments led the yellow fever
commission .to expect. The experiments
of Dr. Caldaz have not begun.
One of the Non-Immunes Bitten by
an Infected Insect Dies of the
Yellow Fever.
SURGEONS DISCONTINUE
THE MOSQUITO TEST
The University of California, with all its
Â¦ invaluable collections, the results of ages
or research, and its millions of dollars in
property, must depend largely, in spite
of a private water system, upon Berke
ley's fire-fighting apparatus and water
pressure for protection from fires. "Al
though the town may not in a legal sense
be required to send, its department to uni
versity fires, yet morally it is. The univer
sity has a reservoir in a canyon west of
the grounds the water in which is for flre
.fighting purposes only. The reservoir is
generally kept full and holds 300,000 gal
lons. An eight-inch main carries the water
into the grounds and past the Chemistry
building, where the pipe is reduced to six
inches, and near the gymnasium to four
inches. At least two hydrants are avail
able for each building and two and a half
inch hose sufficient to reach wherever re
quired is kept at different places. The
University in Danger.
part of North Berkeley are two-Inch and
all through the residence district sur
rounding the university a four-inch main
is the largest. The pipes vary in diameter
in different parts of the town from three
quarters of an inch to twelve inches. West
Berkeley is supplied with water through a
four-inch main down : Unl\-ersity avenue.
It has been the custom of the water com
pany to shut off the heavy pressure on
this pipe at night. This custom is declared
to be the 'reason why the fire in the Xie
haus mill was not extinguished shortly
after it originated.

The department does not possess a
steam fire engine and the only water pres
sure is a direct one from the hydrants. At

all that could be secured. Before the Ala
meda "Water Company was absorbed by
the Contra Costa Water Company the
largest main in town was a six-inch pipe
extending along Shattuck avenue. Re
cently larger pipes have been put In Rose
street to Shattuck avenue, along Shat
tuck to Milvia, Mllvia to Virginia. Virginia
to Grove and along Grove to Center. At
the beginning the pipe is twelve inches in
diameter but diminishes to eight and then
to six inches In Grove street. The pipes
through the scenic tract and the eastern

having a foreman. But there is no chief
and each company is independent. The
firemen receive no pay for their services.
The apparatus consists of hose wagons,
hook and ladder trucks and one chemical
engine. This summary includes one new
and first-class hose wagon and a consider
able quantity of substantial hose. The
remainder of the fire fighting appliances,
the town offlciajs acknowledge, vary from
the moderately serviceable to the prac
tically useless. Some of the companies
pull their apparatus by hand, some
have horses and others borrow teams
when they can get them. The volunteer
firemen receive both praise and con
demnation for the work they do, but their
ability or lack of ability is not material,
considering the difficult conditions under
which they work.

Tlie Fire Department.
Berkeley has seven fire companies, each
Tho people of Berkeley are seriously
alive to the conditions, for they realize
that not only is the present state of af
fairs a constant menace to their homes
but to the State University. What to do Is
the Question citizens and officials alike
are now discussing. The former look to
the latter for action and the latter look
to the former for funds. 'Berkeley is
forging ahead in improvements, in
cluding the construction of many mag
nificent homes throughout the town and
the ever increasing number of manufac
tories on the bay front, but it is feared
this building up and beautifying of the
oity will suffer a serious setback unless
something is done immediately to afford
more efficient fire protection.
|Tr vv ERKELET, Aug. 18.â The thriving
l__2/ town of Berkeley has long since
yV outgrown Its fire department The
. 1 sJ $150,000 fire which destroyed the
big planing mill plant of the
Nlehaus Brothers Company and the
Dverding Starch Works in West Berkeley
Thursday night has brought forcibly be
fore the townspeople its appalling lack
of fire fighting apparatus and its Inade
quate water supply. The fact that Oak
land sent two engines to the fire saved
many times the value of the property de-
Ftroyed and even this timely succor would
have been rendered f utlle by lack of water
had not the tide been running full, thus
enabling the engines lo pump from the
bay.
The real end of the South African war is
not yet in sight and the fact that Â£100,000
In gold was brought in from the Rand
during July has done but little to offset
the feelings of tax-payers, who have spent
over Â£5.000,000 on the war during the same
period, with the prospect of continuing the
same indefinitely. Barring unforeseen de
velopments of an unfavorable nature, it
is probable that a healthy upward move
ment is rot far off: but the likelihood is
that several false starts will be made be
fore this movement is fairly inaugurated.
Stocks were generally higher and busi
ness took a wider range. The attendance
upon the exchange is still meager, owing
to the holiday, but there are indications
of legitimate buying for Investment in the
case of gilt-edged securities, Including
American railroad shares. This movement
is accepted by many as indicating: that the
tide had finally turned and that better
times ar,c ahead after a long period of
stagnation.
Signs are not wanting, however, that the
professionals are making a preconcerted
effort to work up a boom in all sections
of the Stock Exchange, leading off with
Kaffirs on the strength of a few gold ship
ments already received; but conditions do
not favor such a movement. The steel
strike is still a dark cloud over the Amer
ican section.
LONDON, Aug. IS.â The Stock Exchange
settlement last week passed easily. Money
was quite plentiful, so much so as to In
duce talk of a reduction of the bank rate
to 2Vs. But conservatives argue against
this disappointing outlook that there Is
certain to be a gold drain toward the
United States In mid-September, when the
twenty-six and a half millions reserve will
not be too large to meet demands.

WEATHER CONDITIONS AND GENERAL
FORECAST.
Fair weather prevails in the interior of Cali
fornia. Elsewhere west of the Rocky Moun
tains the weather in srenerally cloudy an 1
threatening. Thunderstorms with-high west to
north winds have occurred over Idaho, Nevada,
Utah and. Arizona, and conditions are favor
able for their continuance over these States
to-nizht and Monday.
The pressure changes have been slight In all
districts. .
The temperature has fallen frorh,J to 10 de
grees over the Pacific slope, except, along the
coast of California. â¢ â¢
Forecast made at San s Franclsco for 30 hours
ending midnight, August 19, 1901: -â¢

ably scattered showers in the Sierras; fresh
westerly wind.' Â¦ â¢â¢ " Â¦ " .. â¢ â¢

tered showers In the mountains; fresh west

â¢ Nevadaâ Generally cloudy, with occasional
thunderstorms; brisk to high west wind.

cept foggy In the morning; fresh west wind.

PROPOSALS for Pommel Slickers, Depot Quar-
termaster'8 office, 36 New Montgomery St.,
San Francisco, Cal., August* 16, 1901.â Sealed
proposals, in triplicate, will be received at
this office until 10 o'clock a. in., Tuesday,
August 27, 1901, Pacific Standard Time, and
then opened, for furnishing Yellow Oilskin
Pommel Slickers. Fish brand or equal. Bids
will be opened for the delivery of the same
articles at either the San Francisco, Boston,
Chicago and Philadelphia Depots, and are for
the same for which bids will be opened at
the other depots. To avoid all possible mis-
understanding as to the exact nature of the
slickers to be supplied, bidders will carefully
examine sample slicker at this depot, as un-
der no circumstances slickers inferior thereto'
will be accepted. 'Unguaranteed bids, and
bids for delivery at other points than those
designated herein, will under no circumstances
be entertained. Proposals for less quantities
than advertised for will be entertained. Early
deliveries are essential, and bidders must
etate in their proposal the rate and time of
deliveries, should contract be awarded to
them. Preference will be given to articles of
domestic production and manufacture, condi-
tions of price and quality being equal (In-
cluding in the price of foreign production and
manufacture the duty thereon), and such pref-
erence will be given to articles of American
production and manufacture produced on the
Pacific Coast, to the extent of the consump-
tion required by the public service there. The
United States reserves the right to accept" or
reject any or all proposals or any part there-
of. Information and blanks for proposals will
be furnished on application. Envelopes con-
taining proposals will be indorsed,^'Proposals
No. 7571," and addressed to MAJOR OSCAK
F. LONG. Quartermaster U. S. Army, Depoc
Quartermaster.

It needs more than the simple "say so"
to skip gracefully from the legitimate to
the vaudeville stage, easy as the step
seems to be. Vaudeville has its geniuses
as particular and imperative as any re
quired on the legitimate stage, and r.oi
by^any means to be regarded with the
condescension usually bestowed upon its
every feature (save the financial) by the
legitimists. Last night Louise Thorndyke
Bouclcault. she of the v.-:ilowy form and
blonde frowze, who couldn't and who
wouldn't ever, ever, ever enter vaude
ville, made her debut therein at the Or
pheum. neither to the particular advan
tage of the bill or her own reputation.
Mrs. Bouclcault is concerned in the pro
duction of a little comedy by Augustus
Thomas, "A Proper Impropriety," that is
bad or good according to its handling. It
fell pretty fiat last night, though Her
bert Pattee, Mrs. Boucicault's support,
worked overtime in his desire to liven
things up. The star herself, is out of ber
element. There is no'time in a twenty
minute turn for serpentine posings, whore
the American Bernhardt is at her best,
and snap, vim, salt, 'go, are all lacking
in., her performance. She has not the
patois, accent, pitch of the variety stage,
andjis even wanting in voice to filPthe
large hall.

even despite the comparative failure of
one turn, and also In spite of the fact

The next month or so will see many
changes la the personnel of the Southern
Pacific Company's executive staff. Pres
ident Hays had just about surrounded
himself with men of his own selection and
everything looked lovely for a continu
ous run of five years at $65,000 a year
with never a thought of politics.. 'Now
comes a change in the land. The execu
tive positions of the future will without
doubt be held by men friendly to H. E.
Huntingdon,- and every incumbent who
fails to meet with favor in the nephew's
eves must hunt a job elsewhere. These
changes will take place and until they are
made there will be considerable nervous
tension in local railroad circles.
When the rumor of Hays' resignation,
by its refusal to be silenced, began to re
ceive the consideration of officials whose
right to the yellow jacket ctapends on
presidential sunshine, there" was consid
erable uneasiness all down the line. It
was the uneasiness of suspense, a sort of
hanging in the air which would have to
continue until the suspended one had dis
covered a safe alighting place. The Call
has shown them how the land lies, and
some of them started yesterday on the
Â¦work cf digging trenches and building
fences in preparation for the new state of
affairs. 7. :.: V7 *
Samuel Morse Felton; though not known
personally to the local railroad family,
is well known by reputation. His per
sonality has been felt wherever he has
governed and the fame of . it has long
eincs reached San Francisco in a general
sort of way.
Although rumors of the resignation of
President Hays from the Southern Pa
cific Railroad have . been freely circu
lated during the last two months the an
nouncement of the name of his successor
made exclusively yesterday in The Call,
caused a big stir in railroad circles.-

The Sheridan brought over from the
Philippines and Japan 1244 soldiers and
civilians in the steerage, 12 stowaways, 39
prisoners and 113 cabin passengers. Of the
Steerage passengers 7S2 are men of the
Fourteenth Infantry, 57 of the first bat
talion of the infantry, 16 casuals, 43 dis
charged civilians and 273 discharged sol-,
dlers. N. Feigel, the butcher of the Sum
ner, got left at Nagasaki and came home
on the Sheridan. Three discharged sailors
came home, from Nagasaki, and also sev
en men from Yokohama who missed the
Sumner. â¢

Nagasaki and Yokohama. She was'report
ed as having sailed from Nagasaki on
July 27,. buf no word came through of her
stop at Yokohama. In consequence the
vessel was deemed overdue, and every
body, was wondering what was the matter.
Instead of being overdue, however, she
made'a new-record for the transport ser
vice of 13 days 17 hours from Yokohama
to San Francisco. ;
